==PAGE:4==
Abstract
In the pursuit of sustainable housing within the harsh climate and island economy of
Greenland, the analysis of all aspects of the building envelope is required. In this thesis,
the wall-to-wall, wall-to-floor, and wall-to-roof, both warm and cold, linear building enve-
lope connections for previously identified sustainable timber wall designs are examined
for their hygrothermal performance in DELPHIN software, with thermal bridging contex-
tualized in full-building analysis using IDA ICE. The wall-to-wall connections considered
were: 2-Stud-, 3-Stud Conventional-, and 3-Stud U-style constructions, with additional 50
mm and 100 mm of external rigid insulation for all styles. The wall-to-floor connections
considered were: Platform-, Joist Hanger-, and Balloon-style constructions. Addition-
ally, the Platform-style was considered with an insulating or I-profile rim board, while the
Joist Hanger-style was considered with insulating concrete or attached directly to one of
the wall types. Finally, the wall-to-warm-roof connections considered were traditional, no
overhang, and rafter hanger, while the wall-to-cold-roof connections considered were tra-
ditional truss and energy truss, both with and without an overhang. The warm roof side
connections were also considered with the final rafter running along the side or top of the
side wall studs.
Following the thermal analysis, the thermal bridging variations did not affect the building
energy performance enough to warrant choosing any style of connection over another;
the worst range was between the Balloon and Joist Hanger-style wall-to-floor connec-
tions with only a 3% increase in heating cost. The wall type without a ventilated facade
was determined to be unsuitable in all the connections, while the remaining wall types
were very close to being at risk of mold growth at external locations in the connections.
Additionally, there is an issue of moisture build-up on the roof sheathing of the roof side
connections with a rafter on top of the studs so the inside rafter configuration is recom-
mended. Overall, with the exception of the roof side connections, it is concluded that the
ease of construction and material costs should be the driving factors for these connec-
tions, although further moisture performance analysis is still necessary.

Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
Arctic regions provide numerous challenges related to the design and construction of
buildings. This includes not only the harsh climate, with low temperatures, limited day-
light in the winter, and almost constant daylight in the summer, but also major logistical
difficulties. The construction of houses in Greenland requires the consideration of all of
these to differing levels depending on the specific location.
Due to its vast area, Greenland spans several different climate categories, with the north
being considered High Arctic, most of the middle and south considered as Low Arctic,
and some of the south even being Sub Arctic [1]. The Greenland Building Regulations
therefore split the country into two different climate zones, with the dividing line being the
Arctic Circle at a latitude of 66°33’ N [2]. This project will therefore be narrowly focused
on the climate of the town of Sisimiut on the west coast of Greenland, located just north of
the Arctic Circle as highlighted in Figure 1.1. The ice-free coastline moderates Sisimiut’s
climate somewhat, with the Danish Meteorological Institute (DMI) reporting climatological
standard normals for mean air temperatures of approximately -11 °C in the winter and
about 7 °C in the summer; this open water, together with sea winds, also results in signif-
icant periods of fog, especially in the summer, and an annual average relative humidity of
72%. It does not result in a lot of precipitation, however, with around 550 mm falling on a
yearly basis. Furthermore, while Sisimiut experiences midnight sun for most of the month
of June, there is no period of full polar night with no sun for 24 hours [3].
Figure 1.1: Sisimiut Location in Greenland [4]
As with every settlement in Greenland, Sisimiut can be considered part of an ”island econ-
omy”, with no land infrastructure connecting it to any other settlement in the country [5].
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
1
==PAGE:12==
There has been discussion surrounding a road connecting Sisimiut to Kangerlussuaq,
which is the next nearest major settlement and also contains an international airport, but
as of now this road has not been constructed. This means that everything must be shipped
to the city primarily by boat to the ice-free port, and small amounts by air to the local airport.
This has several knock-on effects related to the construction industry in particular.
Although timber is the main construction material for single-family homes in Greenland,
no trees can be grown there, so all wood-based materials need to be shipped by boat
from Denmark. This not only greatly increases the cost of supplying the materials to a
job site, but can also impact project timelines - for example, the wood may sit uncovered
on location for several weeks before being used. The other main structural material is
concrete, of which the aggregate can be locally sourced, although the cement and rein-
forcement components will still need to be shipped; concrete is therefore used only in the
foundation of single-family homes, but has more prevalent use in larger constructions to
limit the possibility of moisture problems [6]. The third major material to consider is insu-
lation, especially as sustainable building trends considerably increase the amount to be
used. Of special note is rigid insulation, which is ideal for building envelope performance,
but is incompressible, leading to significantly higher transportation costs compared to the
compressible batts and blown insulations.
There has been significant research into improving the sustainability of housing in not
only Greenland, but the Arctic in general, with the ultimate target of the passive house
standard, as with all sustainable constructions. Although a perfect passive house is likely
impossible to implement in the harsh arctic climate of Sisimiut, a significant reduction of
energy use remains a goal. A previous study by Tom Sven Siegemund identified several
sustainable timber construction styles for the exterior envelope of single-family homes in
Sisimiut and analyzed their hygrothermal and economic performances [7]. As suggested
by Siegemund, it is necessary to move toward a holistic full-building analysis to determine
a completely sustainable solution. If one assumes relatively typical exterior roof and slab
constructions, it is therefore prudent to next analyze the connections between all of these
building envelope elements.
This project will begin with a brief summary of Siegemund’s findings before moving on
to an explanation of how the various connections will be analyzed for their hygrothermal
performance. A description of the connection designs will then be presented, divided into
wall-to-wall, wall-to-floor, and wall-to-roof connections, and subdivided based on the wall
type from Siegemund they work with. The raw results will be presented before the thermal
performance is contextualized with the impact on full-building heating requirements, while
the moisture performance is compared to a mold growth model. Finally, the results of the
analysis will be discussed to draw some conclusions on the most sustainable possible
connections, including a brief discussion on their economic viability based on decreased
energy use and the complexity of the connections.
2
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:13==
2
Background Information
As this thesis uses Siegemund’s project [7] as a starting point, the pertinent information
and results of his investigation are summarized here; all information in this chapter is
directly from this thesis unless specifically referenced otherwise. Six different wall con-
structions are presented by Siegemund, of which four will be carried forward into this
project; these are summarized in the following sections. It should be noted that these
four designs were not widely used in the Greenlandic construction industry at the time of
Siegemund’s project, but were presented as improvements to the current practice to meet
the recently updated building requirements. In the years since this project, Masanti has
implemented various variations of their wall design into new single-family constructions
in Sisimiut, while the CLT system has been used in some multi-family constructions in
Nuuk [8]. The two wall types that are not being brought into this project are those named
GBR 2006 and Fibra by Siegemund. The GBR 2006 wall represented the current building
practices in Greenland, so, due to this project focusing on new constructions over reno-
vation strategies, it does not make sense to include the existing practice that does not
meet the new regulations. Meanwhile, Siegemund’s results regarding the Fibra wall were
inconclusive due to a lack of information about the fibreglass material properties, so this
assembly style cannot be considered in this project.
2.1
GBR 2020
This GBR 2020 wall represents a direct evolution of the country’s current building practices
that would meet the new 2020 Greenlandic Building Regulations. It consists of two stud
layers with unbroken insulation in between; the full construction assembly is summarized
in Table 2.1 and Figure 2.1.

The resulting U-value of this wall was calculated as 0.097 W/(m2∙K) using a hand calcu-

analysis concluded that relative humidities (RH) of greater than 80% occurred within the
walls during the winter, which could be solved by moving the vapour barrier to the inside
of the first insulation layer. This threshold for relative humidity was used throughout the
project to predict favourable conditions for mold growth.
Form an economic standpoint, the GBR 2020 wall was found to have the lowest total cost,
with shipping, material, and labour costs all included. It also specifically had the lowest
shipping and material costs. These facts were true even if the insulation thickness was
increased or decreased within the wall assemblies, which was done to compare the costs
at different U-value levels.

3
Hygrothermal Analysis Methodology
In order to complete a thorough hygrothermal analysis of all the required connections, the
analysis will be split into two parts. To determine the linear thermal bridging coefficients,
2D steady-state heat transfer analysis will be performed using DELPHIN 6.1 coupled heat,
moisture, and matter transport software from The Institute of Building Climatology at Dres-
den University of Technology. Following that, a 2D dynamic coupled heat and moisture
transfer analysis will be performed to determine mold growth risk. It should be noted that
the definition of the ”connection” area, compared to the ”building envelope” area, will gen-
erally be cuts following the interior faces of the building envelope elements. What this
looks like specifically for each connection will be highlighted when they are presented in
Chapter 4. From a workflow and results presentation standpoint, it was decided that the
GBR 2020 wall represented the base case, meaning the major different connection styles
will be presented for the GBR 2020 wall, then the best performing connection thermally is
carried across the wall types for comparison. Additionally, any unique connection styles
in each wall will always be included.
3.1
Controlled Variables
To facilitate the comparison of all the connections, several variables will need to be kept
constant. As with all hygrothermal analyses, the boundary conditions are the most im-
portant parameters to determine. In this project, these will be slightly different depending
on the type of simulation (steady-state or dynamic), with the dynamic simulations using
actual climate data. This hourly weather data from a test reference year in Sisimiut is
summarized graphically in Figures 3.1 and 3.2; it is being used instead of the recom-
mended worst-case conditions presented by the building regulations for a more accurate
prediction of possible moisture issues.
Furthermore, the internal conditions for the dynamic simulations will be based on weekly
averages from hourly measured living room conditions at a student dormitory in Sisimiut.
This is assumed to represent typical temperature and RH variation in a relatively recently
constructed building and so similar to what might be expected in a new house. These
weekly averages are summarized in Figures 3.3 and 3.4.
8
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses

Figure 3.2: Exterior Hourly RH - x-axis is fraction of a full year (annum)

Figure 3.3: Interior Weekly Average Temperatures - x-axis is fraction of a full year (annum)
Figure 3.4: Interior Weekly Average RH - x-axis is fraction of a full year (annum)
Similar data was available from the bathrooms in the student dormitory, but this input was
not considered within the scope of this project for several reasons. Firstly, the increased
ventilation rates in the bathrooms mean that any possible RH and temperature spikes
would not last long enough to make too much of a difference to the analysis. Additionally,
there would be different constructions to consider, with tiled flooring and in-floor heating
for example, the added complexity of which should be considered in a separate project.

transfer coefficients retrieved from Rode et al [9].

In addition, DELPHIN requires the discretization of sloped surfaces, which requires the
surface transfer coefficients to be normalized over the resulting increased distance. With a
chosen roof slope of 1:2, this results in external coefficients of 18.3 W/(m2∙K) and 11.2E-

Furthermore, the DELPHIN software requires the input of an initial RH and temperature for
all of the materials in the wall assembly. For the steady-state simulations, this temperature
is 1 °C while for the dynamic simulations it is 20 °C; the default RH value of 80% is kept for
all simulations. However, for the thermal analysis, the dry thermal conductivity specified
in DELPHIN is used, so the initial RH has no effect.

3.2
Thermal Analysis
The main output of the thermal analysis of these connections is a linear heat loss coeffi-
cient Ψ, which has the unit of W/(m∙K). As can be supposed from the unit, this coefficient
represents the heat transfer through the connection per unit length and unit temperature
difference. In order to understand the derivation of this coefficient, several values must
be defined. The thermal resistance, R, of a material undergoing 1-D heat transfer is given
by Equation 3.2.
R = d
λ
(3.2)
Where d is the thickness of the material in the direction of heat transfer and λ is the
material’s thermal conductivity. The total 1-D thermal conductivity of an assembly of ho-
mogeneous materials, also known as the U-value due to it having the symbol U, is given
by Equation 3.3.
U =
1
ΣR
(3.3)
However, in timber construction, there are usually layers that are not homogeneous; stud
layers for example. To determine the equivalent homogeneous thermal resistance of such
layers, Equation 3.4 is used, which is known as the ”parallel-path method”.
Rlayer =
ttotal
∑n
i=1
ti
Ri
(3.4)
Where n is the number of different materials, or ”parallel paths”, in the inhomogeneous
layer, ti is the thickness of each material in the direction perpendicular to the direction of
heat flow and ttotal is the total perpendicular distance being considered.
In a 2-D model such as the connections being considered in this project, the heat flow
rate per unit length, Φl, can be defined with Equation 3.5.
Φl = L2D ∗(θi −θe)
(3.5)
Where θi and θe are the internal and external temperatures respectively and L2D is known
as the ”coupling coefficient” between the internal and external environments.
As mentioned earlier, the thermal analysis of the connections in DELPHIN will be steady-
state 2-D heat transfer simulations with a 1°C temperature difference between the interior
and exterior. A simulation of the entire 2-D profile of each connection, and a length of
building envelope on either side, will output a value of heat transfer (per unit length),
which can be seen to be numerically equal to the coupling coefficient when there is a
1°C temperature difference. The required length of building envelope adjacent to the
connection was determined according to the provisions in DS/EN ISO 10211:2017 [11].
To then transform this value into a linear heat loss coefficient of the connection itself,
Equation 3.6 is used.
14
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:25==
Ψ = L2D −
n
∑
i=1
U ili
(3.6)
Where n in this case is the number of different building envelopes attached to the connec-
tion being analyzed, Ui is the thermal conductivity of each building envelope in the model,
and li is the length of each building envelope included in the 2-D model. However, just as
the building envelopes may have layers that are inhomogeneous, it is possible that the
connections will also contain variations along, for example, the length of the building. This
is solved with a simple weighted average of the different sections, shown in Equation 3.7.
Ψw =
∑n
i=1 Ψiti
ttotal
(3.7)
Where n in this case is the number of different profiles in the connection, Ψi is the linear
heat loss coefficient determined from a separate simulation along each of these different
profiles, and the values of t follow the same definition as in 3.4. These Ψ or Ψw values,
apart from being compared to one another, will be used as an input for the full-building
context outlined in Section 3.2.1.
For practical purposes, these equations can be simplified into two DELPHIN simulations
for each connection (or profile therein), one containing the full construction and one with
the connection area removed. The difference between the outputted heat flux values of
these two simulations is the linear heat loss coefficient of that connection or profile. In
order to approximate steady-state conditions, each of these simulations are run for an
in-software calculation period of 10 days with the specified constant conditions; the final
outputted hourly heat flux is then considered the steady-state heat flux.
The simulation models were further simplified in areas where there were repeating non-
homogeneous layers that did not change the connection design at any analysis plane.
These were substituted with equivalent layers of the same thickness and a thermal con-
ductivity value calculated using Equations 3.2 and 3.4 to decrease the number of simula-
tion runs required for each connection.
Additionally, each full construction simulation will output a field of temperature values for
the connection at the final time output. This temperature field is transformed into a gra-
dient using PostProc 2.2 software, provided by the same institute as DELPHIN, to create
a visual representation of the thermal bridging in each connection. Additionally, these
gradients provide guidance as to where possible moisture build-up might occur, and thus
where to analyze RH variations in the dynamic simulations.

as thermal bridging inputs into IDA ICE 4.9.9 full-building energy simulations. The building
used for these simulations is shown in Figures 3.5 to 3.7.

3.3
Moisture Analysis
The basic process of water vapour diffusion through a homogeneous layer is governed
by Fick’s Law, shown in Equation 3.8:
Gv = Aδpv,x −pv,y
d
(3.8)
Where Gv is the diffusion flow of water vapour, A is the surface area under consideration,
δ is the vapour diffusion resistance of the layer, d is the thickness of the layer, and pv is
the water vapour pressure on each side of the layer. These water vapour pressures are
related to previously mentioned RH values with Equation 3.9:
RH =
pv
pv,sat
(3.9)
Where pv,sat is the saturation vapour pressure of the air at a given temperature, given by
Equation 3.10:
pv,sat(θ) = 288.68(1.098 +
θ
100)8.02
(3.10)
Where θ is the temperature in °C.
Many typical hygrothermal analyses may consider an RH limit of 100% to see how much,
if any, condensation occurs with the wall structure. For this project, however, a lower
threshold of RH will be used to predict the possible growth of mold within the connections,
which can then lead to structural damage of timber elements. The critical RH (in %), RHcrit,
where mold growth may occur is given by Equation 3.11 from Viitanen & Ojanen [12]:
RHcrit = −0.00267T 3 + 0.160T 2 −3.13T + 100.0
(3.11)
Where T is the temperature in °C. This equation is also only valid for temperatures less
than or equal to 20 °C; the critical RH is 80% above this temperature.
18
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:29==
Using DELPHIN to simulate the connections allows for the analysis of this vapour diffu-
sion through complex two-dimensional geometries, as well as including the interactions
between temperature, RH, λ, δ, and moisture storage within each material. As mentioned
3.1, these functions are provided within the files in Appendix B, but in general the thermal
conductivity increases with moisture content, while moisture storage increases with RH.
At a pore level, the vapour permeability decreases with increasing moisture content, while
the liquid conductivity increases; there is no liquid transport considered through insulation
materials or air gaps. Furthermore, it should be noted that all of the connections are con-
sidered completely air tight, so no air transport is considered in this project; ice formation
is also not considered.
From a practical standpoint, the same DELPHIN model geometries were carried over
from the thermal simulations, except with the materials and other controlled variables
as detailed in section 3.1 and without the equivalent homogeneous layers; separate cut
profiles through these layers were simulated instead. Hourly temperature and RH values
were outputted at locations next to timber elements, partly determined by the temperature
profiles created from the thermal simulations. For each connection, these locations are
shown on the drawings presented in Chapter 4. These dynamic simulations are run for
one year of simulation time as a worst-case scenario that includes the drying out of initial
moisture within the structure. The DELPHIN outputs of temperature and RH can then be
compared to the RHcrit to determine the amount of time during the year that the chosen
points in the connections are at risk of mold growth, as well as the longest unbroken period
of time the point is above RHcrit. These results will be presented in what is known as an
isopleth chart, where the temperature and RH of each hour is plotted against each other
and compared to a curve representing RHcrit. It should be noted that, for the calculation
of the longest continuous critical period, one hour below the RHcrit is all that is required to
end the period. The consequences of this method will be analyzed further in Chapter 6
after the results are presented. To be at risk of mold growth, a location needs to have a
relatively unbroken period of 5 weeks (840 hours) above RHcrit [12].
Due to the discretization necessary to approximate the sloped roofs, the DELPHIN sim-
ulations done for the wall-to-roof connections containing slopes had to be altered to a
simpler steady-state analysis in order for results to be acquired within the project time-
frame. These simulations consisted of constant internal conditions of 23 °C and 80% RH
and external conditions of -30 °C and 80% RH; the conditions represented average and
worst-case conditions for internal and external respectively. As the simulations are only
10 days long to approximate steady-state, there is no time for any drying period of ini-
tial moisture. Consequently, the initial RH and temperature set for the simulations were
35% and 20 °C respectively, which can be considered as approximate equilibrium with
the internal conditions. The results of the simulations are RH gradients, which will be
used alongside the temperature gradients from the thermal analysis to visually estimate
the mold growth risk within these wall-to-roof connections.
Finally, after initial analysis of the moisture results suggested the initial moisture content
is either trapped or not able to dry out in the one year considered in the normal simula-
tions, several five-year simulations were completed. The climate data was assumed to
repeat every year, and the initial RH was also varied between 80% and 50% for additional
comparison. The results of these simulations were simply analyzed by graphing the RH
every hour over the simulation length, along with the critical RH every hour, to visually
determine if the connection is drying out over time and to confirm the initial conditions
eventually converge.
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
19
==PAGE:30==
4
Connection Types
This chapter presents all of the connections that will be discussed throughout the rest of
this project. It should be noted that this is just a selection of the total group of connections
that were analyzed and was chosen to facilitate a discussion of the trends found in the
results. The remaining connections are provided in Appendix E for reference. As briefly
mentioned in Chapter 3, the following figures include indications of the area considered
as the ”connection”, shown as an orange dashed line, and the points where moisture
analysis was completed, shown as numbered green dots; these numbers correspond to
entries in the results tables presented in Section 5.2. Additionally, a three-letter code is
provided next to the name of each connection, which also corresponds to the labels in the
results tables in Section 5.2.
4.1
Wall-to-wall Connections
4.1.1
GBR 2020
It should be noted that the assumed default vapour barrier location for this wall is the in-
ner configuration behind the sheathing, shown with the solid blue line, as suggested by
Siegemund [7] to fix possible moisture issues in the original wall he tested. The other con-
figuration, with the vapour barrier outside the inner stud line, will be referred to as ”outer”
henceforth; this location is shown with the dashed blue line. A notable downside of having
the vapour barrier on the interior side of the studs is that it limits the use of the stud line as
an installation space without numerous penetrations being made through the vapour bar-
rier. The external air barrier is easily kept continuous around all the corner connections,
although the common problem of penetrations through the air barrier to attach the facade
battens exists here, but that is more of an issue with the wall design than the connection
design.
Figure 4.1 shows the 2-Stud wall-to-wall connection for the GBR 2020 wall. It consists of
the final structural studs lined up with with internal edge the non-structural and a single
additional stud flush with the outside of the corner to attach the facade battens. This is
essentially the minimal amount of timber that could be used to construct a corner, with the
downsides that a corner drywall clip is required to attach the internal sheathing, and the
one stud making facade attachment slightly crowded.
Figure 4.1: GBR 2020 2-Stud Wall-to-Wall Connection [GWA]

extends to the first non-structural stud, also for anchoring purposes. In either of the 3-Stud
connections, the latter will not be the case as the extra stud provides the additional anchor
point in the corner construction itself. The other complication of the exterior insulation is
the necessity of longer anchoring nails to attach the facade battens to the corner studs,
however the extra thermal bridging of these connectors is beyond the scope of this project.

4.2
Wall-to-floor Connections
4.2.1
GBR 2020
Figure 4.9a shows the platform-style floor end connections for the GBR 2020 wall. The
studs are shown lined up with the joists, although if the spacings specified in Chapter 3 are
strictly followed, this would not be possible along the entire length of the wall and would
thus require a doubled bottom plate and/or extra blocking between the joists; this situation
is not considered in this project. The joists also extend all the way to the rim board and rest
on a single plate on top of the foundation wall. With the chosen vapour barrier location in
the floors, it is not possible to extend the floor sheathing all the way to the rim board like
in typical platform framing while maintaining a continuous vapour barrier. The solutions
to this are either to stop the sheathing at the inside face of the wall, which may require
some unique nailing patterns to transfer lateral forces to the foundation, or to move the
vapour barrier to the internal side of the sheathing. It would also be difficult to keep the
outer vapour barrier location continuous as a different construction workflow would be
necessary to have it under the bottom plate. It should be noted that the combination of
moving the floor vapour barrier inward and the external wall vapour is not considered in
this project. Additionally, as with all the connections to follow in this section, the air barrier
is not kept continuous through the connection. This means special attention must be paid
to sealing the wall air barrier at the bottom of the wall behind the flashing, and the floor
air barrier at the foundation wall. Figure 4.9a also shows some of the possible alterations
for the platform connection: a full wall-width bottom plate which is only possible with one
vapour barrier location, a rim board made out of an I-joist, and an idealized insulating rim
board made from extruded polystyrene (XPS), which is not really structurally possible with
current materials but is used as a theoretical limit.

Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses

The joist hanger style wall-to-floor connections for the GBR 2020 wall are shown in Fig-

the top flange of the joists is in line with the bottom plate. To ensure easy connection of
both the hangers and the wall studs, the joists should not line up with the studs, and if
the spacings result in them lining up, the joist would need to be moved slightly to ensure
constructability. As the connection detail now includes part of the concrete foundation,
the thermal performance of the connection could possibly be improved by adding a layer
of insulative concrete material to the top of foundation wall to match the depth of the floor
joists. The material that was initially considered was lightweight expanded clay aggre-
gate concrete, although autoclaved aerated concrete was used instead as this material is
provided in DELPHIN and similar enough to test the idea. This additional material would
complicate the construction method, requiring either two concrete pours or the connection
of the cast-in-place concrete with precast blocks made from the insulating concrete. This
connection idea is not shown on a separate figure, but is indicated with the hatched area
on the foundation wall in Figure 4.9b.
The final wall-to-floor end connection for the GBR 2020 wall is the balloon-style connection
shown in Figure 4.10a. The joists bear for 200 mm directly to the single bottom plate in
between the studs which are also connected to this bottom plate. The outer vapor barrier
location is not considered for this connection type because continuity is almost impossible

For all the CLT wall-to-floor connections, the continuity of the vapour barrier presents an

Wall-to-roof Connections
4.3.1
GBR 2020
The warm roof end connection for the GBR 2020 wall is shown in Figure 4.16a. The rafter
is cut flat and extends into a vented soffit as an overhang after bearing on either stud
line. As the warm roof is unventilated, this venting should not be continued into the roof
construction. The air barrier is continued up from the wall and is sealed at the bottom of the
roof sheathing to ensure this; the soffit could be removed for the warm roof which would
make this easier as shown in Figure 4.16b, there just needs to be a sufficient drip edge
to drain water off the roof. The vapour barrier in the default location is easily continued
from the wall into the installation space of the warm roof; the outer wall vapour barrier
location is geometrically possible but would required installation before the rafters, which
is unlikely.

The main difference between the warm roof and cold roof connections is the need for
ventilation to be carried into the attic space using baffles between the structural trusses.
In all cases, these baffles are considered to be made out of XPS attached between the
rafters or studs, with a minimum ventilation depth of 50 mm.
In order to create a ventilated soffit in the traditional cold roof end connection shown in
Figure 4.18a, the truss bears on the outer stud line and extends to form an overhang. The
baffles are continued down the inside edge of the soffit to attach to the wall air barrier and
make it continuous in between the trusses. The default vapour barrier location can be
kept continuous from the wall to the roof, but the outer location is once again very difficult
to achieve.

the baffles unless it is possible to remove it. It is much easier to make the air barrier
continuous in this detail, but ventilation would have to be ensured with larger holes in the
facade at regular intervals, which might get filled with snow.

considered with 600 mm and 1200 mm rafter spacing.

4.3.4
CLT
Figure 4.26a shows the warm roof bearing onto the CLT wall with no overhang. Even
though the studs are not structural in this wall, they are considered to be able to carry the
small extra area of the roof sheathing when they are extended all the way up. The roof
vapour barrier can be attached to the CLT wall next to the end batten in the installation
space. In a similar detail, rafter hangers can be attached directly to the CLT wall, which is
shown in Figure 4.26b. The only difference between this and detail CRB is that the CLT
wall extends to the roof for the entire length of the building.
(a) CLT No Overhang Wall-To-Warm-Roof End
Connection [CRB]
(b) CLT Rafter Hanger Wall-To-Warm-Roof-End
Connection [CRC]
Figure 4.26: CLT Wall-To-Warm-Roof End Connections
The energy truss is shown in Figure 4.27a bearing onto the CLT wall with no overhang.
This detail has similar issues with ventilation access as the other cold roof details without
an overhang, but as the studs are bolted to the CLT, there is no conflicting top plate if the
baffles are connected between the studs.

when they are extended to the sheathing.

This chapter presents the results of the analyses done on the connections presented
in Chapter 4. The full results, including those of the additional connections detailed in
Appendix E, are provided in Appendix C and D for thermal and moisture analysis results
respectively. Additionally, as there are several different cross-sections included in each
connection, other letters are added to their letter code to indicate the cross-section; the
added letters and their meanings are outlined in Table 5.1.

5.1
Thermal Analysis Results
In this section, the thermal analysis results for all connections are presented. This includes
numerical results of Ψ from DELPHIN simulations, zone heating from IDA simulations, and
this zone heating as a percent decrease of the baseline heating values presented in Table
3.8. These results are also presented graphically in each subsection, colour-coded by wall
type as noted, with the connection results plotted with round markers and the parametric
run results plotted with triangular markers with a dashed linear trendline. Additionally, a
temperature contour is provided for a selection of the connections to show the thermal
bridging effects. As these contours were generated under the 1°C temperature difference
for the reasons outlined in Chapter 3, the contours can be read as a percentage of the
total temperature change across the building envelope.

5.2
Moisture Analysis Results
This section presents the selected results of the dynamic moisture transfer results. The
tables within this section summarize the hours above RHcrit at each location within each
connection, as well as the maximum periods above RHcrit at these same locations. The
location numbers correspond to the numbers in the connection diagrams in Chapter 4,
while the connection type is referenced with its three-letter code plus the cross-section
code from Table 5.1.
For each location, an isopleth graph of RH compared to temperature is also provided
showing the hourly conditions at each location over the course of the year. In these
graphs, the hours below RHcrit are shown in orange, while the hours above RHcrit are
shown in blue; RHcrit itself is plotted in green. For any location with periods of time above
RHcrit, an additional graph is provided showing the lengths, in hours, of the various pe-
riods, with periods above RHcrit shown in blue and below shown in orange. The long
periods below RHcrit at the start and end of the year (over the winter) are typically re-
moved from these graphs to show the critical summer periods with greater clarity. As this
misconstrues the total hours above and below RHcrit, an additional pie chart is provided
with these graphs to show the full fraction of these two environmental conditions over the
entire year.
5.2.1
Wall-to-wall Connections
The following tables summarize the numerical moisture transfer results of the wall-to-wall
connections. As discernible from the presented figures in Chapter 4, the CLT wall acts as
the vapour barrier in those connections, so they only appear in Table 5.8. Furthermore,
only GBR 2020 walls are shown in Table 5.10 because they are the only wall type with a
possible outer vapour barrier location. Figures 5.8 to 5.12 show the isopleths and periods
for selected connections from the table. The minimal differences evident in the values
between the different vapour barrier locations, which will be discussed further in Chapter
6, mean that these graphs are only shown for the default vapour barrier location for each
connection. For similar reasons regarding very minimal visual differences, graphs are
only provided for the connections that have isopleth or period graphs that are observed
to have unique trends, which will be discussed in Chapter 6. The remaining graphs, as
well as the numerical and graphical results for all the connections that were not shown in
Chapter 4, are provided in Appendix D.

6
Discussion
6.1
Thermal Analysis Discussion
6.1.1
Wall-to-Wall Connections
Looking at the GBR 2020 wall-to-wall connections in Table 5.2, it is noticeable how close
the thermal results of the different connections are to each other. This is despite adding
50 mm and 100 mm of rigid insulation in connections GWB and GWC, which slightly
increases the thermal bridging based on these results, likely due to the increased density
of the rigid insulation; despite the attempts to make the simulations approximate steady-
state, it is still technically a transient simulation where this can have an effect. Also, one
of the corner studs had to be extended to facilitate the attachment of the rigid insulation,
which would offset some of its thermal benefits. Meanwhile, the addition of an extra stud
in the connection from GWA to GWD and GWG has a very minimal effect as well, with
the 2-Stud and 3-Stud U connections resulting in the exact same Ψ value, which was the
lowest Ψ result of all the wall-to-wall connections.
Extending this to the other wall types in Figure 5.1, it can be seen that the connection
results are tightly clustered within each wall type, with any differences having very little
impact on the annual zone heating. What should be noted as well is that the zone heating
results are specific to the chosen low-rise single-family house. As such, in this case, the
length of the wall-to-wall connections are significantly shorter than the roof and floor lo-
cations running along the house perimeter, so the effect of changing Ψ in the wall-to-wall
connections is less than in the other two; this becomes even more obvious when com-
paring Figure 5.1 to Figures 5.3 and 5.6, especially the relative slopes of the parametric
trendlines.
As the 2-Stud connections are the best performing wall-to-wall connections that exist for all
the different wall types, they are compared to each other and it can be seen that GBR 2020
has the lowest Ψ value, and thus largest decrease from baseline zone heating, followed
in order by CLT, Scanwo, and Masanti. Even without looking at the connection designs
again, it can be determined from the order that that is also the order of the amount of timber
present within each connection. This is most significant in the Masanti connections, where
it was assumed that the final rafter would run along the inside of the side wall, meaning
the final structural stud overlaps with the connection; this assumption was maintained
for the GBR 2020 and Scanwo walls, it is just the most apparent with the large Masanti
structural studs. This is beyond the scope of this project, but it is prudent to note that a
different assumption for this would change the designs, as well as the results. It is also
interesting to note that the order of Ψ exactly follows the order of the U-value of the wall
types themselves.. If a certain type of construction resulted in a lower U-value, it is logical
that carrying this construction through the wall-to-wall connection would result in a lower
Ψ value.
The one unique wall-to-wall connection present for the Masanti wall was the 2-Stud Small
connection, where the larger corner stud suggested in the reference typehouse plans was
replaced with a 45 mm wide member. This did not cause a significant decrease in Ψ value
or annual zone heating.
The CLT wall has two unique connections, the 2-Stud Ex and 2-Stud Cut. Although the
2-Stud Ex has the lowest Ψ value of any of the CLT wall-to-wall connections, little change
76
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:87==
in zone heating is experienced. This a good example of the minimal effect changing the
Ψ values of the wall-to-wall connections has on the overall thermal performance of the
entire house.
As mentioned when the temperature contours were presented in Section 5.1.1, the small
variations in Ψ values within each wall type mean there are minimal differences between
the temperature contours as well. The GBR 2020 connections that are presented show
the 2-Stud connection with two thicknesses of rigid insulation, and the main difference is
the physical movement of the studs inward to warmer areas of the wall. This risks bringing
timber into areas above 0°C, or even near the dewpoint temperature of humid indoor air,
which could then lead to moisture issues in the wall. The contour shape also somewhat
changes with the addition of the external insulation, with warmer areas extending outward
through the external bridge of the outer studs, seen in Figures 5.2b and 5.2c, compared
to colder areas extending inward through the same thermal bridges in Figure 5.2a.
The influence of the thicker unventilated facade in the Scanwo wall can be seen in Figure
5.2d with the larger area of low temperature on the external side of the wall. The insulated
installation space on the internal side of the wall pushes the inner stud line into a danger-
ous area that could often be near the dewpoint of warm and humid interior air, which is
generally why insulation on the internal side of the structure is not recommended. The
general shape of the Scanwo contour is otherwise very similar to GBR 2020, with slightly
more frequent variation caused by the offset studs. By comparison, the Masanti con-
nection in Figure 5.2e shows how a non-insulated installation space would behave, with
warmer temperatures at the internal side of the studs. However, the shape of the temper-
ature contour in the corner itself is relatively similar, and the same can be said for the CLT
connection presented in Figure 5.2f. It can therefore be said that the Ψ variation of a level
seen in Table 5.2 for wall-to-wall connections are not immediately evident in temperature
contours.
6.1.2
Wall-to-Floor Connections
Looking now at the GBR 2020 wall-to-floor end results in Table 5.3, the first notable thing
is that the changes in the rim board done to the platform connection in GFB and GFC
do not really improve the thermal performance of the connection at all. The difference
between the full plate and the split plate is slightly more significant, causing an increase
of 32 kWh over a year. The joist hanger connections are significantly worse, with GFG
being the worst performing connection by a wide margin, with an increase in annual zone
heating of 423 kWh over the best performing connection, GFI. The addition of an insulating
concrete layer at the top of the foundation for connection GFH brings the connection closer
to the performance of the platform and balloon connections, but is still at least 59 kWh/yr
worse than them. The balloon framing style connection results in the lowest Ψ and annual
zone heating, 48 kWh/yr better than the platform framing style of GFA.
Analyzing these results graphically in Figure 5.3, the wider range of these connection
results is very evident, as is the greater impact of Ψ changes with the steeper slope of the
parametric trendline. It is also noticeable that the wall-to-floor connections have a much
higher decrease from the baseline annual zone heating compared to the wall and roof
connections. There are two possible explanations to this. The first is that the ”typical”
value of this thermal bridge is out of date in the IDA software and should be updated to
reflect current building trends. The second is more likely, which is that IDA assumes this
connection is a foundation wall to concrete slab-on-grade. This would naturally result in
a higher Ψ value compared to the Greenlandic style of building, with an insulated floor
over an exterior crawlspace. It can be seen that the External Wall/Internal Slab Ψ value in
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
77
==PAGE:88==
Table 3.7 is 0.0686 W/mK, which is significantly closer to the results of this project, which
adds credence to this idea.
Comparing the balloon-style connections across the different wall types (GFI, SFF, MFF,
CFJ), it can be seen that the GBR 2020 wall has the lowest Ψ value, followed in order by
CLT, Scanwo, and Masanti; this is also the order of the decrease in baseline heating and
the order if blocking is added to the CLT connection. The zone heating decrease for the
CLT connections is closer to the performance of GBR 2020 than would be expected when
looking at the Ψ values alone. This is likely due to the influence of the increased thermal
mass of the CLT walls compared to the other wall types in the IDA models. In Figure 5.3,
the CLT parametric trendline has the lowest slope, while the remaining trendline slopes
follow the wall U-value order. This means that the increased heat transfer through the
connections from a higher Ψ value does not have as big of an effect in the case of the
CLT walls because of their ability to retain heat over the course of a day, especially in
the shoulder seasons and summer with a regular amount of solar gains in Greenland.
It should be noted that this behaviour is not seen over the smaller ranges in the other
graphical results in Figures 5.1 and 5.6, so it cannot be considered to have a very large
effect on the results.
The Masanti wall can also be considered with a stud spacing of 1200 mm compared to the
default 600 mm considered in this project, which impacts the balloon framing results seen
in MFG compared to MFF in Table 5.3. With just this spacing change, the annual zone
heating is decreased by 10 kWh, which compounds on the obvious material savings; this
will be discussed more in Section 6.3.
In addition to the blocking, which would likely be added to both the balloon and platform
framing in the CLT walls, CFI is a unique connection with the joists hanging directly from
the CLT wall. This brings the Ψ value very close to the AAC joist hanger connection with
a much simpler construction, although it is still significantly worse than the balloon-style
connections. As mentioned, the blocking decreases the performance of the connections,
with CFK having 63 kWh more zone heating than CFJ, although it is likely a more feasible
construction.
Looking at the wall-to-floor side connections in Table 5.4 does not reveal any further trends
in addition to what has been discussed for the end connections. In all cases, the side con-
nections have a slightly higher Ψ value than the corresponding end connection although
they are relatively close. It should be noted at this point that it is not necessarily the most
accurate to have applied all of the floor connections for the entire perimeter of the building
in IDA rather than where they would actually be located. This means that comparing the
zone heating between the side and end connections is impossible as the higher Ψ values
of the side connections would be for a shorter distance than the lower end connection val-
ues. Physically, the higher Ψ values of the side connections make sense simply because
the final floor joist exists for the entire connection length rather than only every 800 mm
in the end connections.
In the temperature contours for the GBR 2020 wall-to-floor end connections in Figure
5.4, the insulation cross-section of the different connection styles look very similar to GFII
in Figure 5.4b except for the joist hanger connection in Figure 5.4a, where the thermal
bridge is very apparent. The autoclaved aerated concrete in GFHI makes the temperature
contour very similar to GFII. The joist and stud cross-sections for the balloon connection
in Figures 5.4c and 5.4d definitely have an effect on the temperature contours, as would
be expected for these thermal bridges, with the stud having more of an effect. Looking at
the insulation cross-sections across the wall types, the insulated installation space in the
78
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:89==
Scanwo wall is obvious, but the actual thermal bridging differences are not.
6.1.3
Wall-to-Roof Connections
Focusing now on the GBR 2020 wall-to-warm-roof end connections presented in Table
5.5, the GRA connection can be seen to be significantly worse performing than the other
three, which notably do not contain overhangs. The large continuous thermal bridge of
the rafter penetrating the exterior wall to produce the overhang causes a significant in-
crease in Ψ compared even to GRC, where the rafter stops at the facade. As it has been
assumed that the warm roof does not require ventilation, the vented soffit is not a neces-
sary inclusion for the warm roof connections. It is interesting that moving the rafter even
further inside for GRB and GRD does not have a major effect, which is likely due to the
assumption that the exterior stud line would have to be extended to the roof sheathing to
provide structural support, as well as to attach the facade battens for the entire building
height.
Comparing the ”inner no-overhang” connections (GRB, SRD, and CRB) across the wall
types, the Ψ values from lowest to highest are ranked GBR 2020, CLT, and Scanwo, which
again follows the U-value of the walls as with the other connections. However, as was
discussed with the wall-to-floor connections, the CLT connection has an equal decrease
in baseline zone heating as the GBR 2020 connection despite a higher Ψ value, but the
Ψ values are very close in the first place.
The exterior stud version of the no overhang connection for the Scanwo wall can be seen
to be slightly worse than the interior stud version. This is very logical because bearing on
the exterior stud removes the layer of insulation acting as a thermal break in the corner,
which is not offset by the stud not continuing to the roof. However, this improvement
is only equivalent to 13 kWh/yr or 0.1% of the baseline zone heating, which is not very
significant.
The Masanti wall connections are unique because of the hollow stud design allowing for
the rafters to connect inside each stud. This means a direct comparison with the other
walls does not provide much insight, but the Ψ values fall between the Scanwo and CLT
walls and so are overperforming relative to the ranking of wall U-value. The spacing
increase from MRC to MRD causes a slight decrease in the connection Ψ value, and a
22 kWh decrease in annual zone heating.
The rafter hanger connection for the CLT wall is likely a more structurally feasible option
than the corresponding connections in the other walls, but it can be seen to be significantly
worse thermally than CRB. The reason for this is the continuous CLT wall, which extends
to the roof for the entire length of the building rather than at 600 mm intervals as would
be the case in the other walls. This results in a major thermal bridge for the entire length
of the building, and this is reflected in the significantly worse performance.
Moving to the GBR 2020 cold roof end connections in Table 5.6, it can be seen that
the removal of the overhang does not have the same effect that it did in the warm roof
connections; the results for GRE and GRF are essentially the same. Additionally, with
the necessity to vent the attic spaces in the cold roof, removing the vented soffit might
cause some difficulties in achieving appropriate ventilation. The two energy truss sizes
result in very similar Ψ values that are both significant improvements on the value for the
traditional cold roof without an overhang. This improvement is evident without considering
the decreased insulative performance when the mineral wool is compressed in traditional
cold roof constructions, so the actual improvement may well be even more. However,
the removal of 50 mm of timber for the connection in GRN compared to GRI does not
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
79
==PAGE:90==
result in much of an improvement in thermal performance. Neither does adding a small
amount of extruded polystyrene insulation to the rafter cross section to fill the gap left
by the baffles in GRO, with very minimal Ψ and zone heating improvement. As with the
warm roof connections, moving the rafter bearing to the inner stud for GRN compared to
GRQ results in a small Ψ improvement, but does not translate to any major improvement
in zone heating.
Comparing the ”inner no overhang” connections for the smaller energy truss across the
wall types (GRN, SRS, and CRK), it can be seen that the GBR 2020 connection has the
lowest Ψ value, followed in order by Scanwo and CLT, although all the values are pretty
close. However, while the CLT wall has the worst Ψ value in this set of connections, it
once again has a similar decrease in baseline zone heating, but the Ψ values are already
very similar. Additionally in the CLT wall, the studs are considered non-structural in the
wall design, but are assumed to be able to support the extra roof sheathing length in
the ”no overhang” style connection. If this is not the case and the studs would need
to be larger to accomplish this kind of connection, then the thermal performance would
decrease accordingly.
For the Scanwo wall, moving the smallest energy truss to the internal stud causes a small
decrease in both the Ψ and zone heating values. It is a slightly more significant change
than in the GBR 2020 results, but is very similar to the similar connection design change
in the Scanwo warm roof end connections, as would be expected.
Once again, the Masanti wall creates unique connections as it is assumed the trusses
would be attached the same way a rafter would in the warm roof connections. As such,
the Masanti connections are worse than the rest of the energy truss cold roof connections,
and are therefore closer to the GBR 2020 traditional cold roof connections. Again, the
increase in spacing of the rafters has as small effect on the Ψ and annual zone heating
values.
Looking at all these results graphically in Figure 5.6, the worse performance of the Masanti
connections is evident, as is the range of results in between the wall-to-wall and wall-to-
floor results. The trendline slopes are similar to the wall-to-floor ones, so the impact of
changing Ψ is similar in these two locations; the order of these slopes directly follows the
wall U-values.
Looking at the GBR 2020 wall-to-roof side connections in 5.7, it can be seen that moving
the rafter outward toward the exterior improves the thermal performance of the connec-
tion. This is because of the reduction of timber within the connection if the rafter is used
to support the edge of the roof compared to extending the studs to the roof. Compar-
ing the best performing connections across the wall types, the GBR 2020 wall has the
best Ψ value, followed in order by Masanti, Scanwo, and CLT. Scanwo performs badly in
this case because the tongue-and-groove timber siding increases the amount of timber
present in the connection compared to the Masanti non-structural wall. The CLT con-
nection performs poorly because it was assumed that the I-profiles in the wall could not
support the rafter, so the furthest outward the rafter can be placed is on the outside edge
of the CLT wall itself. This also requires the extension of the studs to the roof for overhang
support, which decreases the thermal performance.
6.2
Moisture Analysis Discussion
6.2.1
Wall-to-Wall Connections
Looking at the GBR 2020 wall-to-wall connections in Tables 5.8 to 5.10, and in fact most
of the wall types, the main notable point is that the only location with periods of time
80
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:91==
above RHcrit is location 2 at the exterior side of the corner. Both the total hours and the
maximum periods are decreased by moving the exterior studs inward in GWB and GWC
with rigid exterior insulation. The addition of 100 mm of rigid insulation decreases the total
hours below the 840 (5 weeks) considered as a limit in this project in all the vapour barrier
locations. As the critical area in these connections is on the exterior side, it can be seen
that the location of the vapour barrier has very little effect on the number of hours above
RHcrit. Finally, connection GWG has a lower total hours above RHcrit than GWA and GWD
most likely simply because of the additional timber member placed on the external side
of location 2, which further suggests the migration of vapour from the exterior.
Although only one of the GBR 2020 connections has a total number of hours lower than
840 at location 2, none of the connections have a maximum period anywhere close to this
number, with GWA, GWD, and GWD the worst at 143 consecutive hours above RHcrit.
However, analysis of these periods requires a look at Figure 5.8. The isopleths for location
2 are very similar shapes except for GWG, which has a more consistent RH level due to
the low vapour permeability of the additional timber member. Also, the isopleths for GWB
and GWC are moved lower on the RH axis compared to GWA, and are compressed
slightly on the temperature axis, both of which result in the lower amount of hours above
the RHcrit line. The period graphs reflect the variability of the external RH, with many
switches between drying and wet throughout the year. Looking specifically at the GWA
periods, it can be seen that there are multiple long periods above RHcrit during the middle
of the year in addition to the 143 hour long one reflected in the tabular results. This results
in a period closer to 500-600 hours without any major drying period during the summer
time, which is still below the limit of 840, but is much closer to being an issue than the
143 hours would suggest. The decrease in the amount of these longer periods is evident
in Figures 5.8d and especially 5.8f, where the two longest critical periods, which only add
up to about 90 hours, are broken by a longer drying period. Connection GWG has a lower
amount of period variation compared to GWA, again indicating the impact of the extra
timber member. However, although the total hours above RHcrit is lower than GWA, the
total of all the critical periods during the summer is very similar between GWG and GWA.
Looking at the 2-Stud connections across the wall types, specifically GWA, SWAI, MWAI,
and CWA, the critical periods all occur in location 2 on the exterior side of the corner. All
of the other wall types have a higher total hours above RHcrit than GBR 2020, ranked from
lowest to highest as Scanwo, CLT, and Masanti, although they are all relatively similar,
and the batten cross-section for Scanwo is significantly worse. The maximum periods do
not follow this same ranking, instead following GBR 2020 as Masanti, CLT, and Scanwo.
Looking at the Scanwo wall-to-wall connections in Tables 5.8 and 5.9, it is notable that
the batten cross-section has significantly more hours above RHcrit and a longer maximum
period compared to the insulation cross-section at location 2. As the connections without
a vapour barrier are slightly worse again, it is not the case that the batten is blocking
drying toward the interior. What is likely occurring is the initial moisture within the batten
is diffusing outward due to the vapour pressure difference caused by the temperature
difference, and then building up behind the unventilated facade. This phenomenon is
validated by the slight increase in critical hours in SWAB without a vapour barrier, as
there would be less resistance for the moisture to diffuse outward without a vapour barrier
in place. The isopleth graphs for location 2 for both of the cross-sections, seen in Figures
5.9a and 5.9b, show a much more consistent RH than the corresponding locations in
the GBR 2020 connections, which is because of the thick unventilated facade resisting
vapour diffusion through it, so the RH does not vary with the external RH as much. Also,
looking at the period graph for SWAB in Figure 5.9c, there are three additional periods
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
81
==PAGE:92==
above RHcrit without long periods of drying, which brings the total critical period to around
1100-1200 hours, which is above the limit of 840 hours and definitely a concern. Although
not quite as bad, the same can be said about the longest six periods of SWAI, which is
more of a concern given that the insulation cross-section makes up a larger percentage
of the total height of the wall so any mold problem would be more widespread. In general,
it appears that the Scanwo connections are not able to dry out the initial moisture content
within the wall.
The results from the two Masanti wall-to-wall connections presented in Tables 5.8 and 5.9
are very similar to each other, with the smaller 2-stud connection having slightly higher
hours and longer max periods at location 2. Additionally, MWD has some hours above
RHcrit at location 3, although they are only halfway to the limit of 840. The isopleths for
location 2 in both connections are very similar, with a shape as shown in Figure 5.11a.
The location 3 isopleth for MWDI in Figure 5.10a shows the hours above RHcrit due to a
high average RH level. As location 3 is further to the exterior in the small stud connection
compared to the regular 2-Stud, a temperature decrease would be expected, but it is
interesting that the moisture movement seems to be inhibited more with less timber in
the corner. However, it is likely again just the change in location shifting the RH to the
wrong side of 0.8. The period graph for MWDI location 3 in Figure 5.10c is not significantly
variable because of the amount of timber nearby; it is also very obvious that the RH levels
are not significant enough to be an issue. The location 2 period graphs in Figures 5.11b
and 5.10b differ with MWDI having one long period in the middle compared to two slightly
shorter periods in MWAI, but both show the variation expected on the exterior side of the
corner. Although the location 2 maximum periods are only 147 and 293 hours for MWAI
and MWDI respectively, the total of the long critical periods comes very close to the 840
hours, so these connections would be at some risk of mold growth with this level of initial
moisture.
The results of the three CLT wall-to-wall connections in Table 5.8 are also very similar to
each other, all with total hours above RHcrit around 1600 and a maximum period of ap-
proximately 400 hours. The isopleths of CWA and CWE in Figure 5.12 are also extremely
similar, with location 2 of CWE slightly flatter in shape because of the extra external stud
inhibiting moisture transfer slightly. The CWE location 2 period graph in Figure 5.12d
shows this same thing as decreased variation compared to CWA in Figure 5.12c; the total
of the longer critical periods for both are very close to the 840 hours to be considered a
risk of mold growth at with this initial moisture content.
6.2.2
Wall-to-Floor Connections
Comparing the insulation cross-section results of the GBR 2020 wall-to-floor end con-
nections in Table 5.11, the platform connections have a lower total hours above RHcrit at
location 2 compared to the joist hangers and balloon connections, although the insulat-
ing concrete brings this result closer to the platform connections. However, the platform
connections also have a high number of hours above RHcrit at location 5, while GFII has
the lowest critical hours at location 4. None of the connections have maximum periods
above the limit of 840 hours, but this requires further analysis of the period graphs. The
total of the longer critical periods for GFAI location 2 in Figure 5.13c is only around 500
hours, with a similar total for GFHI location 2 in Figure 5.14c. The same analysis done
for GFGI in Figure 5.14b comes very close to the limit of 840 hours, and GFII location 2
in Figure 5.14d is approximately 700 hours; the isopleth graphs for all these locations are
not significantly different, with a shape as shown in Figure 5.14a. GFAI location 5 has the
same behaviour as location 2 in the same connection, which makes sense given they are
both on the outside edge of the corner, just with 2 next to the studs and 5 next to the sill
82
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:93==
plate. Location 4 in all the connections is the inner edge of the sill plate so the periods
vary with the external conditions, meaning there is no stretch of longer critical periods that
could result in some issues when looking at Figure 5.13d.
The other cross-sections of the GBR 2020 balloon connection show other areas of pos-
sible mold issues. The caveat to this is the timber in these other cross-sections unrealis-
tically traps the moisture in these 2D cross-sections when it could migrate along the wall
as well. This is most apparent in GFIS location 6, which is the edge of the interior stud at
the floor level, with a period above RHcrit of 1457 hours. The isopleth of this location in
Figure 5.15c shows this very well, with the RH never falling below 80% and even climbing
to 100%, suggesting there could be condensation occurring at this point. However, the
low hours in location 6 for the other cross sections could suggest there is an ability for this
to dry out along the wall. Other locations with a total higher than 840 hours are GFIS 3
and 5, and locations 2, 4, and 5 for the joist cross-sections. Although none of these have
maximum periods above 840 hours, the two long periods for GFIS 3 in Figure 5.15d add
up close to this limit value, while the two long periods for GFIS 5 in Figure 5.15e add up to
well over 1000 hours. This suggests there is moisture build up occurring in on the inner
edge of the outer stud near the sill plate for this 2D cross-section. This location does not
have the same issues along the joist cross-sections, with the total of the maximum peri-
ods in Figure 5.16e around 600-700 hours. This suggests there is some drying potential
along the wall, although these numbers are relatively close to the limit. The isopleths for
the joist cross-sections, as seen in Figure 5.16b, are also significantly wider, suggesting
the moisture is transported more readily. Location 4 in the joist cross-sections is affected
by the extra timber in the location, but the moisture is still able to dry to the exterior enough
that the total of the longer periods does not get close to 840 for either in Figures 5.16d and
5.16f, although it is worse for GFIJE. Location 2 is very similar between these two cross-
sections as well and the total of the longer periods evident in Figure 5.16c is very close to
the limit of 840 hours, so this area could be close to at risk of mold growth. Overall, this
external location is on the line of being at risk of mold growth in both these cross-sections,
GFGI 2, and GFIS 3, while the only locations with extreme risk being GFIS 5 and 6.
Moving the floor vapour barrier above the sheathing has some effect on the numerical
results, as can be seen in Table 5.13, increasing the total hours above RHcrit at location 2
by around 100 hours. The other locations and maximum periods are not affected as much,
so this suggests the initial moisture in the sheathing dries toward the exterior instead of
the interior with this vapour barrier location.
The different wall types can be compared to each other at location 2 of the insulation cross-
section of the balloon connections (GFII, SFFI, MFFI, and CFKI) as this cross-section
and location is shared throughout. The Scanwo wall performs the best at location 2 by a
significant margin, while GBR 2020, Masanti, and CLT are all very similar.
Looking at the different cross-sections of the Scanwo balloon wall-to-floor end connec-
tion in Table 5.11, the locations with more than 840 total hours above RHcrit are SFFI 4,
SFFOS 4, SFFIS 2 and 5, and locations 2, 4, and 5 for the two joist cross-sections; only
SSFJE and SFFJM location 5 have a maximum period longer than 840 hours. These lo-
cation 5s, which corresponds to the facade at the top of the joist, appear to have possible
condensation problems with isopleths as shown in Figure 5.17b. Additionally, the SFFIS
location 5 period graph in Figure 5.17d has two long critical periods in the summer which
together matches the longer critical periods for SFFJE and SFFJM seen in Figures 5.17f
and 5.18d, well above the 840 hour limit. It should be location 5 in SFFI has significantly
lower total hours above RHcrit as there is no condensation occurring, but these hours

suggest there may not be enough drying potential along the wall to overcome the issues

The Masanti wall-to-floor connection locations with total hours above 840 are MFFI 2 and

suggesting drying potential along the wall. Overall, the outer edge of the bottom plate is
the most critical area, being close to the hour limit in multiple cross-sections, while the
interior of the built-up studs just need to be kept as dry as possible during construction.
Looking at the CLT wall-to-floor end results in Table 5.12, the locations with high totals
of hours above RHcrit are CFDI locations 2, 5, and 6, locations 2 and 3 for CFII, CFKI,
and CFKSE, CFKSM 3, CFKJE 2 and 4, and CFKJM location 2. The only locations with
maximum periods above 840 hours are location 3 in CFKSE and CFKSM. These latter
two locations are the interior edge of the I-profile studs on the bottom plate, so it appears
that the initial moisture is trapped in this area of the wall between the studs and the block-
ing. This is especially evident in the isopleth for CFKSE 3 in Figure 5.20a where the RH
reaches 100% for much of the year and therefore possibly condenses; the corresponding
isopleth for CFKSM 3 in Figure 5.20d is not quite as bad, but is above 80% the whole
time. Both cross-sections have similar major critical periods in the middle of the year as
seen in Figures 5.20c and 5.20e. It also appears there would not be much drying potential
along the wall, with the period graph of CFKI 3 in Figure 5.19g having two major periods
totalling very close to the 840 hour limit. Location 3 for the high joist hanger detail CFII
has a slightly lower total of critical periods seen in Figure 5.19f, and the isopleth similar
to GFIS 3; this location is the outer edge of the CLT wall on the bottom plate for this con-
nection. CFDI locations 2 and 5 are both on the exterior side of the wall and therefore
exhibit very similar behaviour to GFHI 2 and MFFI 2 respectively. Location 6 for CFDI
also comes close to the 840 hour limit when analyzing Figure 5.19e, and the isopleth in
Figure 5.19d suggests limited moisture transfer as would be expected on the inside edge

of the rim board.
CFII and CFKI also display the similar behaviour expected for location 2 at the external

In general, the wall-to-floor side connections have similar results to the corresponding
wall-to-floor end connection results, with the exception of the period graphs presented in

of just over the 840 limit while GFRI 5 is well below this number. The GFRS 5 periods in
