over 1000 hours. Finally CFRI 4 has a critical period total close to 840 while CFRSE 5 is
at a higher risk with a total over 1000 hours. The latter of these two locations represents
moisture being trapped between the two edges of the I-profile stud though, which is unre-
alistic as mentioned before, while the former is next to the bottom of the wall for the entire
width of the building, so could also be a big problem.
Comparing the GBR 2020 connections between Tables 5.11 and 5.15, it is notable that
using a vapour permeable concrete material has minimal effect on the results. The only
two differences worth mentioning are in connection GFGI location 3, where the vapour
permeable concrete removes all hours above RHcrit, and connection GFIJE location 2,
where the maximum period above RHcrit is reduced from 392 to 293 hours. Neither of
these changes have any bearing on whether the connection is at risk of mold growth
though, so overall the results using the moisture tight concrete material can be considered
accurate.
The five-year simulation results presented in Figure 5.22 fall into a couple categories. The
first is the most common, where the initial moisture content has an effect on the first year
or two of the simulation, then the two starting points converge to similar variations; this
occurs in the locations near the exterior like GFII 2 in Figure 5.22a. GFIS 2 in Figure
5.22b also looks to be converging eventually, it is just slowed by the timber in the location;
the same can be said about GFIJE 3, which is not shown. These behaviours suggest the
initial year simulations in the remainder of this report are definitely a worst case scenario,
but the results are still relevant since the lower initial RH climbs to meet the higher level
eventually. However, the remaining locations in GFIS seem heavily affected by the initial
moisture content, as seen in GFIS 3 in Figure 5.22c with the dynamic equilibrium reached
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
85
==PAGE:96==
at different points depending on the initial RH level. This reinforces the idea that moisture
gets trapped by the timber in these 2D simulations, as these locations are between the
two studs, so the initial moisture has nowhere to go along this cut. As mentioned earlier,
this is not realistic behaviour as the moisture can migrate along the wall into the areas
of clear insulation, but does indicate that the members in the construction should be kept
dry. GFIJM 3 in Figure 5.22d is the worst-case version of this behaviour, with the RH
staying essentially equal to the initial RH, suggesting that the initial moisture content of
the timber in the joist is not able to dry from this location. Also of note from the RHcrit
variation is that it is obvious that the hours above this level are occurring alongside the
warmer temperatures in the summer, suggesting moisture diffusion inward, lack of drying
potential outward, or a combination of the two.
6.2.3
Wall-to-Roof Connections
The wall-to-roof side results for the GBR 2020 wall in Table 5.16 have numerous locations
with a total number of hours above RHcrit greater than 840. Two of these locations have
maximum periods longer than this limit: GRSI 5 and GRTS 4. The first of these has an
isopleth similar to GRRI 3 in Figure 5.23b, but the period graph in Figure 5.23f has one
major period of approximately 1500 hours, with a secondary period of around 300 hours
just before it. As this location is the intersection between the rafter and the roof sheathing,
it seems likely that the moisture is trapped between the rafters and collecting at the coldest
point in this span, when it could naturally dry along the roof in the third plane. GRTS 4 is
the same location at the roof sheathing, with the same behaviour occurring. This location
is less of an issue in GRR, behaving like GRRI 2 in Figure 5.23c with a total of the critical
periods equalling around 700 hours. GRRI 7 also behaves like GRRI 2, but with total
periods slightly above the 840 limit; GRSI is exactly the same at this location. GRSI 4
has a similar isopleth as GRRI 3 with less RH variation seen in Figure 5.23e and a critical
period total of slightly above the 840 limit.
The isopleth for GRTI 4 in Figure 5.24a suggests possible condensation issues, but ap-
pears as several longer periods in Figure 5.24c, totalling over 1000 hours. This follows
the issues at the same location along the stud cross-section, so there might not be much
drying period along the third axis. GRTI 3 has a similar isopleth as GRRI 3, with critical
periods totalling around 900 hours when analyzing Figure 5.24b; GRTI 2 and 8 have be-
haviours like GRRI 2, but 8 has a critical period total of around 600 hours instead. The
remaining GRTS locations in Figure 5.24 have isopleths like GRRI 3, but GRTS 3, 6, and
7 have critical period totals of 1400, 900, and 1250 hours respectively, all of which indi-
cate a high risk of mold growth. The first two of these locations are between the two stud
lines, while location 7 is within the timber stud and batten connection. Overall, GRS and
GRT have high mold growth risk along the bottom of the roof sheathing with limited drying
potential along the roof, with some areas between the studs close to being at risk. GRR
is only close to the limit near the facade at ceiling height.
The Scanwo wall-to-roof side connections have a few unique period variations, as seen
in Figure 5.25, but no unique isopleth behaviour. SRWI 6 has similar condensation be-
haviour as GRTI 4, so the 1300 hour period seen in Figure 5.25a is unsurprising. SRWOS
2 and 6 both have similar isopleths to GRRI 5, but the period graphs in Figures 5.25b and
5.25c have critical period totals of 1700 and around 900 respectively. SRWIS 6 is also
very similar to GRTI 4, with the two long periods in Figure 5.25e totalling over 1600 hours;
SRWIS 2 has isopleth behaviour similar to GRRI 3 but the periods total only slightly less
than SRWOS 2. The final location of note is SRWI 2, which has periods the same as
GRTI 4, so is also at very high risk of mold growth. All of these locations are along the
exterior, either right next to the unventilated facade or the end rafter.
86
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:97==
All the Masanti wall-to-roof side connection results followed behaviour presented for the
GBR 2020 and Scanwo connections. The locations of note are location 4 in MRQI and
MRQS, which have similar period variation to SRWIS 6 and GRSI 5 respectively and are
thus at very high risk of mold growth. Location 3 in MRQS is also at very high risk with
similar periods to GRTS 3, while the same location in MRQI is similar to GRTI 3, so close
to the 840 hour limit. Additionally of note is that MRQI 6 was similar to an external wall-
to-floor location like GFAI 2. Overall, the bottom of the roof sheathing in the connection is
at high risk of mold growth, with limited apparent drying along the wall.
There are two unique isopleths presented in Figure 5.26 for the CLT wall-to-roof side
connections. CRNI 6 in Figure 5.26c is an even worse version of GRTI 4, being close to
100% RH for the entire year; the critical period sum of close to 2500 hours in Figure 5.26d

9 with a very high risk like SRWI 6. Overall there are extreme problems beneath the roof
sheathing, with some issues within the stud that likely have a higher drying potential than
evident in the model.
The warm roof end connections for the GBR 2020 wall exhibit similar thermal and moisture
behaviour, represented by GRBI in Figures 5.27a and 5.27b, with the overhang in GRA not
affecting things much along the insulation cross-section considered. At this steady-state
condition, there are no areas with a relative humidity above 80% and 60% temperature
change (which corresponds to 0 °C in the worst-case 50 °C temperature difference). The
stud cross-section in Figures 5.27c and 5.27d has a possible issue on the inner edge of
the outer stud, although still slightly too cold at this extreme condition. The removal of the
vapour barrier and movement of it outside the inner stud line does not drastically change
the RH contours so they are not shown. The one exception is the outer vapour barrier
location creates a much wider dry zone in the stud cross-section seen in Figure 5.27e, so
there is no possible mold growth between the studs.
As the Scanwo warm roof end have similar temperature contours as the GBR 2020 con-
nections, the only contour of note is the RH contour for SRDI, shown in Figure 5.27f. In
this cross-section, the inner edge of the top plate approaches an RH of 80% in a slightly
warmer area that may cause some slight mold growth risk. The Masanti warm roof con-
nection also only has one cross-section of note, with MRCSE shown in Figures 5.28a and
5.28b. The inner edge of the stud within the connection is close to 80% RH in warmer
temperatures, but this does not appear to be occurring in a very large area.
The insulation cross-sections of the CLT warm roof end connections have much larger
areas with an RH of 80% or more, extending into warmer parts of the wall as seen in
Figure 5.28d. This suggests that the CLT wall is not as effective a vapour barrier as the
traditional ones used in the other wall types, with more moisture able to migrate through
the construction. In addition to this occurring at the exterior side of the wall, 80% RH
occurs next to the top of the stud in Figure 5.28e, and the same location in the stud edge
cross-section, similar to the Masanti connection, and next to the CLT wall in the same
location in Figure 5.28f. The latter of these is more of ann issue as this location exists
along the entire length of the structural wall of the house.
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
87
==PAGE:98==
The notable locations above 80% RH in the GBR 2020 cold roof connections are next to
the XPS baffles, which reach 100% RH at points as seen in Figure 5.29b. Although this
area of the connection is cold enough that mold is unlikely to grow, the risk of condensa-
tion and freezing suggests a more vapour permeable material would be a better choice
for baffle construction. Additionally, removing the vapour barrier in the insulation cross-
section in Figure 5.29d creates a huge area above 80% RH. Although there is no timber
in this area within the cross-section, it could result in mold growth in the truss and studs
next to this area in three dimensions. Overall it seems the vapour barrier has a much
larger effect in the performance of the cold roof connections compared to the warm roof
ones; the outer vapour barrier location has minimal effect. The temperature contours are
all very similar to the one presented for GRFI in Figure 5.29a.
The insulated intallation space in the Scanwo wall seems to create some unique RH con-
tours in the cold roof connections, with a couple warm areas near 80% RH. In the inner
stud cross-section in Figure 5.29e, there are two of these locations: next to the heel mem-
ber of the truss and especially just below the top batten in the installation space. The latter
of these represents the risk of insulating on the interior side of the studs, as this area of
the connection is very warm so more at risk of mold growth. The final location at risk is the
intersection between the outer stud and the top plate of the inner studs in Figure 5.29f.
The temperature contours do not differ much from the GBR 2020 connections
The Masanti cold roof connections do not have the risk of mold growth in the installation
space as it is not insulated. The only slightly warmer location where there might be some
risk is next to the heel member of the truss in the stud mid cross-section in Figures 5.30a
and 5.30b.
As with the warm roof connections, the CLT cold roof connections have a larger area
above 80% at the exterior compared to the walls with vapour barriers, but these are still
too cold to be at risk of mold growth as can be seen in Figures 5.30c and 5.30d. It is
interesting to note that the area next to the top of the stud shown in Figure 5.30e is not
near 80% RH like it is in the warm roof connections and the Masanti cold roof connection.
6.3
Economic Discussion
Although an in-depth economic analysis is beyond the scope of this project, it is impor-
tant to contextualize the results in economic sense as this is a major consideration for a
remote community like Sisimiut, and all of Greenland. From a thermal bridging improve-
ment perspective, it should be noted that the current price of heating in Greenland is 0.8
Danish kroner (DKK) for every kWh used for heating [8]. This means that the changes
of less than 10 kWh/yr heating amounts in the wall-to-wall connection results have ba-
sically no economic impact on the homeowner. Material and shipping costs should also
be considered, but the addition of a few extra studs for corner constructions is unlikely
to impact the overall building cost significantly. With this being the case, the two aspects
to really consider for the wall-to-wall connections are moisture performance and ease of
construction.
For the GBR 2020 wall, the lack of installation space would mean drywall clips would be
required to build either the 2-Stud or 3-Stud U corner details, suggesting the conventional
3-Stud detail might be the most straightforward. This connection has very similar mois-
ture performance as the 2-Stud, so can safely be recommended even with the slight extra
material cost. The moisture issues in the Scanwo wall appear to be because of the wall
design itself than any detail design, so will be omitted from this discussion. The Masanti
and CLT wall-to-wall connections are both very close to being at risk of mold growth at the
88
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:99==
exterior edge of the studs, so the investment of 50 mm of rigid insulation might worth it to
mitigate the risk. However, because the results are on this line for this high of an initial
moisture content throughout the wall, further investigation is necessary to determine if this
added complexity is worth it to remove the mold growth risk. The change to the small stud
in the Masanti corner does not affect the results much so the size suggested in their plans
can be used if desired. The external 2-Stud connection for the CLT wall would complicate
construction significantly, and has a small negative affect on the moisture performance,
so this extra cost is not worth it. The holes in the I-profile webs also do very little in this
analysis so would not be worth any extra cost. For both these walls the 2-Stud construc-
tion does not complicate the detail much, so is recommended to save a small amount of
material cost over the 3-Stud details.
The wall-to-floor end connections have a much larger range of heating values, with the
worst performing GBR 2020 joist hanger connection, GFG, causing a 423 kWh/yr heating
increase over the best performing GBR 2020 balloon connection, GFI. This corresponds
to an increase of 338 DKK/yr, or around 3% of the yearly heating bill, which is not sig-
nificant economic incentive for a homeowner to pay more for special constructions to
increase the thermal performance of the building envelope connections. Therefore, the
only benefit of this heating energy decrease is from an environmental perspective if the
home is using a traditional oil boiler for heating. However, this heating system is more
unlikely for a new construction, and using electricity for heating both costs the same and
uses renewable hydroelectric energy in Sisimiut, and many of the larger communities in
Greenland. Material costs could play a larger role in these connections, so this is added
to moisture performance and ease of construction as the major aspects to consider.
The location changes of the vapour barrier within the GBR 2020 wall have minimal neg-
ative affects on the moisture performance, so the location that is easiest to construct and
be kept continuous can be used. This would appear to be above the floor sheathing
and inside the interior stud line, which would also allow continuity in the wall-to-floor side
connections. Re-ordering the construction process to achieve the outer vapour barrier
location in the walls would only be recommended if contractors are not capable of sealing
penetrations in the vapour barrier for installations.
As the existence of the unventilated facade once again appears to be causing the issues in
the Scanwo wall-to-floor connections, it is omitted from this discussion as well. The other
wall types have common possible moisture issues at the outer edge of the bottom plate in
the balloon wall-to-floor end connections. The balloon-style connections are slightly more
complicated to construct than the platform-style connections but with a material savings,
while the joist hangers are relatively similar to balloon-style. It appears in the GBR 2020
wall that the platform-style has less of a problem at this location than the others, so if it
is the more familiar construction style, it would likely outweigh the material costs. The
extreme vapour moisture issues and five-year simulations between studs in all wall types
emphasize the importance of keeping building materials dry, which might add a small cost
during construction to cover them. Finally, while the 1200 mm spacing of the Masanti studs
does not affect a lot of these results, the material savings would be worth it to construct
in this style; the AAC concrete is not worth it in any situation.
The range of GBR 2020 wall-to-roof thermal results is lower than the wall-to-floor end
results, so a similar economic conclusion can be made for them as well. The inner wall-
to-warm-roof side connection is likely the easiest to construct, although the outer top con-
nection would save material over the others. All the wall types have extreme moisture
issues below the roof sheathing in the outer-style connections while the GBR 2020 inner
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
89
==PAGE:100==
connection only has one location close to being at risk. Combining the easier construc-
tion and lower mold risk, the inner construction would be recommended to avoid long-term
costs.
In the wall-to-roof end steady-state results, there are a couple general economic points
to discuss. First, the CLT wall does not justify any increased cost as it is less effective
at retarding vapour transport than a traditional vapour barrier. The insulated installation
space can have some moisture issues in the Scanwo wall, but it would save money and
reduce risk to not insulate it like in the Masanti wall. The extra labour time of ensuring
a correctly installed vapour barrier in a cold roof with no penetrations is definitely worth
the cost, as would the extra cost of an installation space in this roof style to minimize this
risk.
90
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
==PAGE:101==
7
Conclusions and Future Work
7.1
Conclusions
The goal of this thesis was to analyze typical Greenlandic building envelope connections
within the sustainable wall designs previously presented by Siegemund and suggest pos-
sible improvements. Although the breadth of this project resulted in several compromises
to achieve a wide-ranging analysis, some conclusions can still be made from this work.
From the thermal analysis results, the best performing connections were the 2-Stud wall-
to-wall connections, balloon wall-to-floor connections, and 100 energy truss wall-to-roof
connections. However, the different wall-to-wall connections all performed similarly when
contextualized in a full building, while the joist hangers were the only wall-to-floor connec-
tions to have a significant negative impact. Finally, the different energy truss sizes did not
have much of an effect on the heating levels for wall-to-roof connections, and neither did
removing the overhangs; the latter was more impactful for warm roof connections. From
an economic standpoint, the changes in heating levels do not have a huge impact on what
a homeowner would be paying because of the low cost of energy in Greenland.
The risk of mold growth did not change in the different wall-to-wall connection styles,
with all GBR 2020 connections not at risk, while the Scanwo wall had problems and the
Masanti and CLT were close to some risk. The easiest connection to construct is the most
economic choice for these connections. All the wall-to-floor balloon-style connections had
some areas at risk of mold growth on the exterior side of the sill plate, with the Scanwo wall
also at risk behind the facade. The platform-style connection performed slightly better, so
would be the economic choice if contractors were used to that style; otherwise the balloon-
style is recommended for material savings. The outer vapour barrier in the GBR 2020 wall
would be difficult to construct and did not impact the results, so is not recommended.
All wall-to-warm-roof side connections had moisture issues along the bottom of the roof
sheathing with the exception of the inner style connection. This is also the economic
choice for ease of construction, and so is recommended. The steady-state analysis of
the wall-to-roof end connections suggested that the CLT wall is not an effective vapour
barrier, the insulated installation space in the Scanwo wall is a bad idea, and a tight vapour
barrier in the cold roofs is necessary.
Overall, based on the results of this project, the ease of construction and material costs are
more important considerations than thermal and moisture performance when considering
these linear building envelope connection details. The only major exceptions to this being
that the Scanwo wall performed poorly in all moisture simulations, while the inner-style is
recommended for the wall-to-roof-side connections. However, there are significant gaps
in this study that will be discussed in Section 7.2 that could change this conclusion.
7.2
Future Work
Due to the sheer number of variables present in both the connections of residential build-
ing envelopes in general, and specifically the simulations that made up the bulk of this
project, there is still a significant amount of future work that can be done. The first is per-
forming in-situ testing and measurements in the connections of a test house with realistic
climatic conditions. Regarding these climatic conditions, the crawlspace conditions would
benefit from some measurement data from existing crawlspaces, and bathroom data or
Hygrothermal Analysis of Linear Building Envelope Connection Details in Typical Greenlandic Houses
91
==PAGE:102==
data from different housing types (e.g. detached, row-houses, mid-rise apartments) could
be used for internal conditions. Other climatic effects could also be considered in more
detailed simulations, including driven rain, solar heating, and wind-driven air transport.
There are several other variables present in the DELPHIN simulations that could be varied
to capture a larger picture of the hygrothermal performance: the initial RH and tempera-
ture conditions which were found to have a high impact on the results, longer simulations
for more connection types to assess the drying or moisture build-up potential, dynamic
simulations of the wall-to-roof end connections (possibly with another software to better
handle the sloped roof surfaces). There are also numerous different material assumptions
that could be varied within the simulations. Finally, the ideal simulation of these connec-
tions would be in 3D to capture heat and moisture transfer along the axis not considered
in the 2D cross-sections presented in this project as well as to analyze the 3D corners of
the construction. An additional detail that could be added to these 3D simulations would
be point thermal bridges, especially metal components.
The selection of connection designs presented in this project is also far from the complete
set of connections that could be constructed. Finally, the mold growth model that forms
the basis of the analysis is not the only model that could be used. The mold development
index was also not used in this project due to the limitations regarding long drying periods,
so if this was updated it could also be used to do more specific analysis than a simple
hour limit.
